Title
Analysis of Metro Copenhagen’s Bus Transit Reliability and Punctuality

Learning Objectives (Generell):
An MSc from DTU:
• Can identify and reﬂect on technical scientiﬁc issues and understand the interaction between
the various components that make up an issue
• Can, on the basis of a clear academic proﬁle, apply elements of current research at international
level to develop ideas and solve problems
• Masters technical scientiﬁc methodologies, theories and tools, and has the capacity to take a
holistic view of and delimit a complex, open issue, see it in a broader academic and societal
perspective and, on this basis, propose a variety of possible actions
• Can, via analysis and modelling, develop relevant models, systems and processes for solving
technological problems
• Can communicate and mediate research-based knowledge both orally and in writing
• Is familiar with and can seek out leading international research within his/her specialist area
• Can work independently and reﬂect on own learning, academic development and specialization
• Masters technical problem-solving at a high level through project work, and has the capacity
to work with and manage all phases of a project – including preparation of timetables, design,
solution and documentation
Learning Objectives (Project Speciﬁc):
The Candidate from DTU Transport is able to:
• Organize and carry out a project work with a clear structure including project statement, rele-
vant theory, analyses and discussions
• Survey and explain literature within the public bus transport system
• Explain the important key performance indicators in the bus transport transit management
• Review the main concepts of measuring performance in terms of reliability and punctuality in
public bus transport
• Prepare provided MOVIA bus data for detailed analysis by designing a data structure from
methods in the literature
• Formulate an adopted analysis structure based on the literature review
• Synthesize the obtained eﬀects, criteria and indicators for performance in the public bus trans-
port
• Apply and assess statistical tests to evaluate the inﬂuences on the performance of the public
bus transportation in Metropolitan Area Copenhagen
• Interpret and discuss the results obtained from the MOVIA data and conclude suggestions for
improvements the public bus transportation in Metropolitan Area Copenhagen
• Write a technical report in an understandable, well structured and well documented form as
well as an oral presentation
==PAGE:3==
Abstract [english version]
This master thesis analyzes the punctuality and regularity of two selected bus lines, 150S and
173E, in the greater Copenhagen Area.
The aim of this thesis is to derive insights about patterns and causes of delay, which shall be
the basis for further route planning and scheduling optimization. The data is collected by the
automatic vehicle location (AVL) and is prepared, cleaned and enriched for the development
of the analysis.
Six diﬀerent key performance indicators (KPIs) are used in this study to measure the punc-
tuality and reliability.
These are on-time performance in terms of schedule adherence and
headway irregularity, run time variability, bus bunching, average passenger wait and average
passenger travel time. The ﬁrst four are operator oriented reliability measures and the last two
are passenger oriented.
The result of this thesis is a detailed overview of the punctuality and regularity of the two bus
lines. The main ﬁndings of this analysis are that (1) buses on average depart late from their
origin stop, (2) a low performance in regularity and (3) that the trip direction 1 (inbound) is
for both bus lines performing better than trip direction 2 (return).
To conclude the paper makes suggestions for improvements and further research such as (1) the
optimization of layover time, (2) the proposed introduction of a headway-based indicator for
bus drivers that improves regularity and (3) the suggestion of further infrastructural changes
are outlined.
==PAGE:4==
Abstract [danish version]
Dette kandidat speciale analyserer punktligheden og regelmæssigheden af to udvalgte buslinjer,
150S og 173E, i hovedstadsområdet.
Formålet med denne afhandling er at undersøge mønstre og årsager til forsinkelse, som skal
danne grundlag for yderligere ruteplanlægning og optimering. Dataet indsamles af Automatic
Vehicle Location (AVL) og bliver sorteret og bearbejdet til analysen.
I dette studie anvendes seks forskellige Key Performance Indicators (KPI’er) til at måle punk-
tligheden og pålideligheden.
Disse er on-time indikatorer med hensyn til planlægning og
overskridelse uregelmæssighed, kørertids ﬂuktueringer, bus bunching, gennemsnitlige passager
vente- og rejsetid. De første ﬁre indikatorer baseres på operatøren pålidelighedsforanstaltninger,
og de sidste to er passagerorienterede.
Resultatet af denne afhandling er et detaljeret overblik over punktligheden og regelmæssigheden
af de to buslinjer. Hovedresultaterne af denne analyse er, at (1) busserne i gennemsnit altid afgår
sent fra deres oprindelsesstop, (2) en lav præstation i regelmæssighed og (3) at tur retningen 1
(indgående) præstere bedre for begge buslinjer, i forhold til turretning 2 (retur).
Konklusion, forslag til yderligere undersøgelser og forbedringer som (1) minimering af ven-
tetiden, (2) den foreslåede introduktion af en hovedbaseret indikator for buschauﬀører, der
forbedrer regelmæssigheden og (3) forslaget om yderligere infrastrukturelle ændringer er skit-
seret.

==PAGE:12==
1
Introduction
By 2025 Copenhagen will grow by 100.000 new citizens and 20.000 new workplaces that will
lead to more congestion on roads, paths and rails (Jensen and Baykal [2013]). This economic
growth requires good mobility, easy accessibility, safe and fast transport while ensuring cleaner
air and less noise for its citizens.
The Copenhagen City Council set an aspiring goal of being carbon neutral by 2025. One of
the main components to achieve this goal is the mobility target. In the CPH 2025 Climate
Plan Copenhagen set’s the goal, that 75% of all trips shall be made by walking, bike or public
transport (Jensen and Baykal [2013]). Therefore any growth in traﬃc must consist of pedestri-
ans, public-transport users and cyclists. To facilitate this, the City of Copenhagen is working
closely together with various initiatives and suppliers in the public transport sector and putting
considerable eﬀort into providing an integrated, eﬃcient and green transport network (Jensen
and Baykal [2012], kommune [2018]).
1.1
Overview and motivation
With the rising demand for transport, a good public transport network is crucial. Copenhagen is
investing in its infrastructure to ensure a high-quality service, environmentally friendly and cost-
eﬀective service, which is in the interest of all stakeholders in public transportation. Considering
the eﬀects of the growing capital region Zealand, the analysis of the current public transport
system is the basis for the investments and changes introduced in the future.
1.2
Objective and scope of the thesis
This paper is part of the research within the Integrated Public Transport Optimisation and
Planning (IPTOP) project which is a joint project between multiple stakeholders from the
state (Traﬁkstyrelsen - Danish Transport Authority), the public transport sector, such as DSB
(Danske Statsbaner - the Danish railway operator) and MOVIA (responsible for all buses in
Greater Zealand) and research institutions, such as the DTU (Technical University of Denmark).
Within this project, innovative methods for the transport service design and supervision are
1
==PAGE:13==
developed which take advantage of previously unavailable bus and passenger data. The ob-
jectives of the IPTOP project are a balanced improvement in the passenger experience and
operating eﬃciency, and a consequent increase in public transport market share and progress
in the response to climate change, which is in line with the CPH 2025 Climate Plan.
The objective and scope of this paper is the analysis of the data which is supplied by MOVIA
within this project. Insights about regularity and punctuality within the public bus transport
system in Greater Copenhagen shall be generated. The performance of bus lines is analyzed,
patterns and causes for delays are found and shall be the basis for the following route optimiza-
tion, planning and scheduling.
1.3
MOVIA
MOVIA is responsible for the public bus transportation in the capital region (Region Hoved-
staden) and the region Zealand (Sjælland). Almost 210 million passengers a year are being
transported on almost 450 bus lines, 9 local railway lines and ﬁve Flextraﬁk schemes. The
company is owned by the capital region of Denmark, region Zealand and the 45 municipalities
in the coverage area.
Figure 1: MOVIA region: 2 regions and 45 municipalities - Source: MOVIA [2015]
2
==PAGE:14==
The bus transport system has 15.000 stops and a detailed map of all bus stops can be found
in the appendix A. MOVIA is responsible for the bus transport system, however, it does not
operate the buses themselves. The buses are operated by 18 contractors of which the biggest
one is Arriva A/S. In total, all operators run more than 1.200 buses to supply the service to
the citizens. MOVIA has more than 330 employees and its tasks are traﬃc planning and de-
velopment, scheduling, tendering, information and marketing, operational monitoring, ﬁnances
and accounting (MOVIA [2015]).
Figure 2: Bus line 6A in Copenhagen
1.4
Thesis Structure
This thesis consists of 5 major chapters.
Chapter 1, the Introduction, gives information regarding the background, motivation, objective
and scope of this paper.
Chapter 2, Literature Review provides the current state in literature and research about the
public bus transport, the importance of reliability (2.1), diﬀerent perspectives (2.2), key perfor-
mance indicators (KPI) (2.3), causes of variability and unreliability (2.4), as well as strategies
to improve reliability (2.5).
3
==PAGE:15==
After the literature has been presented, the Methodology in chapter 3 is explained. In this sec-
tion, the data collection (3.1.1), selection (3.1.2) and manipulation (3.1.3) process are explained
and the deﬁnitions of the KPI’s that are used in this paper (3.2) are outlined.
Chapter 4, Results and benchmarking of Metro Copenhagen Bus Transport Lines shows and
explains the results of each KPI for the corresponding bus line and summarizes these.
In the last chapter, chapter 5, the limitations of this paper are outlined, the results are concluded
and further suggestions for research are given.
4
==PAGE:16==
2
Literature review
After an introduction about public transportation in Greater Copenhagen in chapter one, chap-
ter two presents a review of the current literature on the public bus transport reliability. Deﬁni-
tions, diﬀerent perspectives, measures and KPIs as well as causes for unreliability and strategies
of improving it are presented.
2.1
Reliability in Public Transport
Service quality in public transportation is according to Murray et al. [2010] deﬁned by 166
attributes. The most important factors are the frequency of the service and the punctuality
which is the reason why this paper focuses on them (De Oña and De Oña [2014]). Among
other important attributes are the comfort and cleanliness, the safety, the availability of infor-
mation, the personnel courtesy and the fare (Murray et al. [2010], De Oña and De Oña [2014]).
Especially in the last years’ reliability has become increasingly important as a key quality and
performance indicator for public transportation (OECD [2010], EuropeanCommission [2011]).
König and Axhausen [2002] conclude that research over the last decade shows that the reliabil-
ity of the transportation system is a key factor regarding the mode choice behaviour of people.
Further surveys and research such as Bajcetic et al. [2018] show that reliability is the most
important aspect of public transport and Paulley et al. [2006] even states that passengers con-
sider service reliability twice as important as frequency. But despite the importance of reliable
transport service, in most countries, passengers do not perceive the services as reliable (van
Oort [2014]).
There are several reasons why reliability is so important to both passengers and operators.
The beneﬁt of a reliable transit system from the passenger perspective is the arrival at their
desired destination at the expected time. An unreliable network results in unpredictability
for the passenger which may result in an increase in waiting time for the passenger (Wilson
et al. [1992]), a lower probability of an on-time arrival at a passenger’s destination (Abkowitz
and Tozzi [1987]), additional passenger travel time and a lower probability of ﬁnding a seat
(Van Oort [2011], Lee [2013]).
The beneﬁt of a reliable network for operators is the likely decrease in service cost. Furthermore,
5
==PAGE:17==
a reliable network also provides a higher level of service which can attract new passengers and
increase revenues (Lee [2013]). An unreliable network, on the other hand, results in unnecessary
expenses, due to a higher probability of having to dispatch additional vehicles or drivers, leading
to increased operating costs (Abkowitz and Tozzi [1987]).
Generally, not only the reasoning for the importance of reliability but also the deﬁnitions
of reliability show diﬀerent interpretations in the literature as well as diﬀerent aspects for
passengers (demand) and operators (supply) of the public transport system.
According to
Wijaya [2009] it is "the ability to provide services promised to the right (accurately) and the
ability to be trusted (dependable), mainly to provide services in a timely manner (on time), in
the same manner in accordance with the schedule that has been promised, and without making
a mistake each time"(Wijaya [2009],p. 15f.).
According to Sorratini et al. [2008], reliability is deﬁned as a measure of the probability of a
trip to take place in accordance with the expected trip elements, such as travel time, comfort
and cost.
The International Transport Forum (ITF) which is an inter-governmental body within the
OECD (Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development) "deﬁnes network reliabil-
ity as being that which provides consistent and, thus, predictable travel times.
Thus, the
network is reliable as long as travel times are consistent, even if the network underperforms
due to regularly-slow speeds caused by traﬃc congestion."(OECD [2010], p.31). Furthermore,
it states "the ability of the transport system to provide the expected level of service quality,
upon which users have organized their activities" (OECD [2010], p.31). In this deﬁnition reli-
ability is deﬁned from both perspectives, the operator perspective (provide consistent and [...]
predictable travel times) and from the passenger perspective (expected level of service quality,
upon which users have organized their activities).
Since the reliability is interpreted diﬀerently from the operator and the passenger the two
diﬀerent perspectives are explained in the following chapter 2.2.
6
==PAGE:18==
2.2
Perspectives of reliability
As already described in 2.1 reliability can be seen from two perspectives. In the following, both
of them will be explained in more detail.
2.2.1
Operator perspective of reliability
From the perspective of an operator the eﬀects of an unreliable service results in unsatisﬁed
customers which may result in fewer customers, because they are looking for and using alter-
native solutions for commuting. Furthermore higher costs occur through extra costs such as
the overtime pay for the bus driver and the dispatch of extra drivers and vehicles (Abkowitz
and Tozzi [1987]).
For public transport operators schedule adherence, headway adherence (the time between two
vehicles passing the same point traveling in the same direction on a given route), and run time
adherence are commonly used measures of reliability (Arhin et al. [2014], Kirnpel et al. [2017],
Levinson [2005]). It is key to improve these KPIs in order to satisfy customers and therefore
proﬁtability.
2.2.2
Passenger perspective of reliability
From the passenger perspective, it is important to have short travel times and good transit
services. Most important however is the reliability of the service.
A lower value in travel time variability may allow passengers to choose later departure times
and on-time arrival at their destination. Vice versa if the service is not reliable, the journey
is frustrating and causes stress and might cause "ripple-eﬀects or, worse, snowballing (com-
pounding or growing) eﬀects, aﬀecting other activities or stages in the personal or logistics
chain, constituting a cost to those involved."(OECD [2010], p.33). The possibility of missing
a connection or late arrival at the destination hinder passengers to plan activities eﬃciently.
Looking into the future these connections are across several services and providers, such as a
bike-sharing, train operator and a bus operator. A customer might for example ﬁrst use a bike
from a bike sharing provider to go to the train station from where the train brings one to the
city center where the passenger switches to a bus for the ﬁnal destination.
7
==PAGE:19==
As of today, passengers deal with travel time variability through various means. One way is
to adjust the departure time (Coulombel and Palma [2012]), change route (Abdel-Aty et al.
[2012], X.Liu et al. [2004]) or mode (Chorus et al. [2006]) or to travel somewhere else. The
worst case scenario is to simply cancel the trip. Due to limited alternatives, the most used
strategy is usually to leave with a safety margin (Bates et al. [2001], Li et al. [2010], Benezech
and Coulombel [2013]).
From the passenger perspective, the travel time variability is the
representative KPI that should be improved.
8
==PAGE:20==
2.3
Measures and key performance indicators of reliability
Because reliability is the most important factor in the public transport system, KPIs have
been developed to measure it. Today the automated collection of data through technologies
such as automated vehicle location (AVL) and automated passenger counters (APC) enables
an easy way for collecting the required information which is needed to calculate the KPIs and
thereupon act on them if they are not satisfying. In the now following, the most important
KPIs, namely on-time performance in terms of schedule adherence and headway irregularity,
run time variability, bus bunching and average passenger wait and travel times as well as other
analyses and KPIs such as dwell time are explained.
2.3.1
On-time performance: Schedule adherence for low-frequency bus services
The on-time performance (OTP) measure depends on the frequency of the service. For low-
frequency bus services, it is described as schedule adherence and is often measured by the
punctuality of arrivals and/or departures at stops (van Oort et al. [2015], OECD [2010]). A
low-frequency service usually follows a schedule and has a headway of more than ten minutes.
Headway is the time between two buses passing the same point, traveling in the same direction
on a given route. It depends however on the operator to deﬁne the range in which the service is
regarded as punctual. A list of the American Public Transportation Association describes the
various deﬁnitions of punctuality for several United States cities (Roberts et al. [2010], wmata
[2018]) and MOVIA [2015] describes it for Copenhagen.
• SFMTA (San Francisco): one minute early to four minutes late
• NYMTA (New York): ﬁve minutes early to ﬁve minutes late
• CTA (Chicago): one minute early to ﬁve minutes late
• LACMTA (Los Angeles): one minute early to ﬁve minutes late
• WMATA (Washington): two minutes early to seven minutes late
• MBTA (Boston): two minutes early and seven minutes late
9
==PAGE:21==
• MOVIA (Copenhagen): one minute early to ﬁve minutes late (at timing point places)
The mathematical description of this OTP is following (see equation 1, van Oort [2014]).

In this formulation, the punctuality is calculated as the (average) deviation from the timetable

this formulation lacks the important indication whether a bus departs early or late. An early
departure has a bigger impact on passengers than a late bus since it leads to a waiting time
equal to the headway (Hsched

equation 1 is commonly used as a measure for OTP.
2.3.2
On-time performance: Headway irregularity for high-frequency bus services
The OTP measure for high-frequency bus services can also be described as headway irregu-
larity and is explained through the coeﬃcient of variation (CoV) of headways or the headway
distributions. A high-frequency service is a bus service with a headway of less than ten min-
utes. Headway irregularity is used as a measure since passengers often arrive randomly and the
headway irregularity can aﬀect both expected waiting times and the variability of passenger
loads. The variability of headways causes travelers to perceive service as unreliable, especially
if two or more buses arrive at a stop after a long gap in service. There is clearly a problem when
buses drive along the route one right after the other (bunching) which results in oversupply in
a segment along the bus route and a gap in service at another segment along the bus route. In
these cases, the available resources are not being used eﬃciently.
The headway irregularity is explained through the CoV of headways. It can be measured at
the route level and at the individual trip level and the formulation can be seen in equation 3
(van Oort [2014]).

In practice, however, not only these statistical measures are used. Similar to schedule adherence
many operators deﬁne a bandwidth for headways in which a bus is considered to be on-time and
publish the results of this measure. MOVIA deﬁnes good regularity as the "distance between
two buses at a given place that does not exceed the planned interval plus ﬁve minutes" (MOVIA
[2015]).
MBTA [2017a](Massachusetts Bay Transportation Authority) in Boston deﬁnes that a bus
leaves its origin and mid-route timepoint no later than the scheduled headway plus three min-
utes.
This means that in a given example there is trip 1 that is scheduled to depart at 8:00 in the
morning and the following trip of that route is scheduled at 8:07, hence the headway is 7
minutes. This means that trip 2 shall not depart any later than 8:12 to be considered on time
12
==PAGE:24==
(planned interval of 7 minutes plus 5 minutes according to MOVIA). In the same example the
bus of trip 1 is actually departing at 8:03, which therefore means that the bus of trip 2 could
leave at 8:15 and still be considered on time, since he arrives 12 minutes (Planned interval of
7 minutes plus 5 minutes = 12 minutes) after bus of trip one.
2.3.3
Dwell time variability
Dwell time variability describes the variance in dwell time of a bus at a stop that is used for
boarding and alighting. Typically dwell times are longer at holding stops, stops where buses
are supposed to stop in case they run before schedule. Also these stops usually connect multiple
bus lines or other modes of transport, such as the metro or trains, with each other.
2.3.4
Run time variability
Run time variability is another important KPI to describe the service quality. It describes the
variance it takes a bus to complete one trip from starting terminal to ﬁnal stop. Bus trip times
diﬀer over the year by month, week and day. This is due to various reasons such as weather
and diﬀering passenger loads. More people use buses for example during rush hours, during the
week or in bad weather conditions. An illustration of an example trip time distribution over a
year is given in ﬁgure 3, on the following page (Van Oort [2011]).
Most public transport providers already handle these variations by applying diﬀerent sched-
ules over the year (for example summer and winter timetables), over the week (weekdays and
weekend schedules) and over the day (schedules during rush hour and non rush hour).
MOVIA also applies diﬀerent schedules and headways depending on the hour of the day and
day in the week (Monday to Friday, Saturday and Sunday or holidays) (DOT [2018]).
By setting diﬀerent trip times for diﬀerent periods, operators take these distributions into
account when setting up the schedule. However, trip time variability still occurs even within
homogeneous periods (e.g. peak hour) in homogeneous periods per year (e.g. weekdays in
November).
13
==PAGE:25==
Figure 3: Example Vehicle trip time distribution over a year - Source: Van Oort [2011]
2.3.5
Bus Bunching
Bus bunching is the event of two or more buses running along the same route, which were
scheduled to be evenly spaced, but instead run in the same place at the same time.
This
occurs when at least one of the buses has a small delay which may provoke a rising number
in passengers at the next stop, which increases the dwell time and might slow down the drive
time which leads to an even more increased delay. The following bus, on the other hand, will
have fewer passengers, shorter dwell times and therefore run faster which potentially leads to
both buses to run right behind each other (bus bunching). Overall irregular headways between
buses lead to longer waiting times for passengers, overcrowding in some buses, and an overall
decrease on the level of service and capacity (Feng and Figliozzi [2011]).
In the literature, there is no unique way of identifying bus bunching. Feng and Figliozzi [2011]
for example uses an arbitrary value of three minutes between two buses at a given point on
a given route. Moreira-Matias et al. [2012] use a more dynamic approach. They identify bus
bunching if there exists a xi satisfying the inequality

2.3.6
Average passenger wait time
Passenger wait times in the context of this paper is described as expected waiting time per
passenger and the average additional waiting time per passenger due to the unreliability of a
bus at a given stop. Since the arrival pattern of passengers depends on the bus line mode (high
or low frequency serviced bus routes), the equations diﬀer depending on the provided service.
Low-frequency bus line
For low-frequency services, it is assumed that passengers plan their arrival at the ﬁrst stop of
their trip according to the schedule. Some passengers plan their arrival earlier, others later,
therefore a random arrival within a range of the scheduled departure time −τearly and +τlate is
assumed. If the vehicle departs within this time window it is assumed that passengers do not
experience any additional waiting time. Research about empirical values of τearly and τlate is
presented in van Oort et al. [2012]. As mentioned earlier, it is important to note that there is a
diﬀerence between driving ahead of schedule and driving late. Driving ahead of the scheduled
departure time leads to a waiting time equal to the headway (Hsched
l
; assuming punctual
departure of the successive vehicle). Driving late leads to a waiting time equal to the delay
ddeparture

(van Oort et al. [2012]).

waiting time will be equal to half the headway.

2.3.7
Average passenger travel time
In general the passenger travel time consists of the time a passenger needs to get to the bus
stop (access time), the waiting time at the bus stop (see previous section 2.3.6), the time in
the bus (drive time) until alighting at the ﬁnal stop and some egress time to reach the ﬁnal
destination (see also ﬁgure 4).
Figure 4: Components of passenger travel time - on the basis of Van Oort [2011]
In this research, the access and egress time will be neglected since these are variables indepen-
dent of the reliability of the bus service.

2.4
Causes of variability and unreliability
The causes of variability in travel time are various. The main inﬂuencing factors are shown in
an overview by van Oort et al. [2015] (see ﬁgure 5). The factors are categorized into external
and internal factors and the travel time variability in terminal departure time variability and
the trip time variability. The trip time variability includes the actual driving time, the dwell
time at a stop and the stopping time. The driving time includes accelerating, braking and
unplanned stopping. The unplanned stopping is the stopping of vehicles when no boarding and
alighting is enabled. This occurs mainly because of other traﬃc and traﬃc lights. The dwell
time is the time used for boarding and alighting of passengers at a stop. The stopping time is
closely related to driving time and is the stopping time of a bus without boarding and alighting
(Van Oort [2011]).
Figure 5: Main causes of service variability in urban public transport - Source: van Oort et al.
[2015]
20
==PAGE:32==
2.4.1
Internal causes
Schedule quality: The schedule may inﬂuence the driver’s behaviour. If the schedule does
not include suﬃcient buﬀer times, some drivers will try to stick the timetable and speed up,
while others will drive as usual and not speed up. This combination of schedule quality and
driver behaviour leads to service variability (van Oort et al. [2015]).
Driver behaviour: Every driver has a diﬀerent driving behaviour. While some are more
aggressive, others are rather passive and the diﬀerences in these driving behaviours result in
variances in speed as well as in stopping and accelerating the bus along the route thus in a
faster or slower journey for passengers. Furthermore, the behaviour regarding the opening and
closing as well as waiting for late arriving passengers inﬂuences the variability (van Oort et al.
[2015]).
Infrastructure conﬁguration: The infrastructure, such as the location and design of stops,
but also the mix of street traﬃc, the turns at intersections, traﬃc control may result in variabil-
ity of travel time depending on the occurring traﬃc. This could lead to delays for buses at stops,
junctions or intersections and therefore in increasing variability (Arhin et al. [2016],Rajbhandari
et al. [2003],van Oort et al. [2015]).
Service network design: The service network conﬁguration includes the length of a route
and number of stops and shared stops on a route. Another example is the synchronization
of lines and schedules, to increase the connectivity and transfers between lines. However, this
dependency of lines may result in greater variability since delays are transferred between lines
(van Oort et al. [2015]).
Other public transport: Also other public transport, such as other buses that are standing
at the next stop but also while driving on lanes or intersections inﬂuence the trip time.
Vehicle design: According to Fernández et al. [2010] a wider door can reduce the average
alighting time by almost 40% and a lower ﬂoor height reduces the average alighting time from
1 to 9%. The positioning and the number of doors also inﬂuence the dwell time at a bus stop
and therefore also the overall travel time (Weidmann [1995]).
Platform design: The design of the platform such as the width, length, location of sheds
and other facilities aﬀect the passenger behaviour and create a better distribution along the
21
==PAGE:33==
platform (van Oort et al. [2015]).
2.4.2
External causes
Weather: Weather is a major inﬂuence on travel time variability since diﬀerent kinds of
weather, mainly rain or snow, result in an adopted driving behaviour. Hofmann and O’Mahony
[2005] show that travel time is longer on rainy days than on non-rainy days.
Other traﬃc: Other traﬃc summarizes cars, bikes, pedestrians and other public transport
like buses. Traﬃc and traﬃc congestion is another major inﬂuence on travel time variability.
The extent to which it aﬀects the variability depends on the level of dedicated public transport
infrastructure, such as dedicated bus lanes (Ben-Dora et al. [2018]).
Irregular loads: Unexpected high loads during rush hour or caused by the “bunching” eﬀect
impact the braking and accelerating of the bus and therefore the overall travel time (Fonzonea
et al. [2015]).
Traveller behaviour: The age of passengers (Tirachini [2011]) and crowding (Currie et al.
[2013]) but also other factors such as carrying luggage aﬀect the boarding and alighting speed.
In other literature, the same causes for travel time variability are found but sometimes in a
diﬀerent categorization. Song et al. [2013] clusters factors into the following ﬁve categories:
passenger factors (e.g.
boarding, alighting time), infrastructure factors (e.g.
number and
location of stops), running environment factors (e.g. weather, traﬃc patterns), river behaviour
(e.g. schedule behaviour) and lastly operation and management factors (e.g. scheduled travel
time).
While it is diﬃcult to inﬂuence external causes, like the weather conditions, it shall be focused
on the main internal causes such as the schedule quality.
22
==PAGE:34==
2.5
Strategies to improve reliability
There are several measures and strategies that can be taken in order to improve reliability.
These improvement measures can be taken on an operational, tactical and strategical level.
2.5.1
Improvements on an operational level
Operational measures have already been developed early on.
Examples are skipping stops
(Koﬀman [1978], Li et al. [1995], Eberlein et al. [1998]), deadheading (Eberlein et al. [1998]),
adding vehicles and vehicle holding. Vehicle holding is a strategy where bus stops are deﬁned
as holding stops. At these stops, a little time buﬀer is included in case the bus is running late.
If the bus is not running late the bus still has to wait until the scheduled departure time and
is not allowed to leave early. Many of these operational measures can only be taken if they
are predeﬁned on a strategic or tactical level. For instance, for vehicle holding certain stops
have to be deﬁned as checkpoints and a certain departure time has to be deﬁned. Furthermore,
Van Oort [2011] argues that vehicle holding depends on the type of schedule (schedule- or
headway-based) and should be considered at the tactical level for optimal results (see also the
following chapter 2.5.2).
2.5.2
Improvements on a tactical level
Trip time determination
In most cases the trip time for the schedule is chosen by selecting a percentile value. For the
public transport in urban areas usually a large percentile value of this distribution (e.g. 85th
percentile) is chosen to determine the planned trip time for the schedule. This ensures that
most drivers can be on time (85% of the bus trips arrive on time). This method in most cases
also inﬂuences service reliability. Due to the high percentile values many trips may leave a bus
stop before the scheduled departure time, thus being an important source for passenger delay.
Changing the parameter of the percentile value will imply changes in the number of buses ahead
of schedule, punctuality and the average additional travel time per passenger (Van Oort [2011]).
Muller [1995] proposed the 85th percentile value in his research, which leads to an achievable
timetable. This results in a design dilemma of service reliability versus schedule attainability.
23
==PAGE:35==
Van Oort [2011] analysis of actual and theoretical data shows that "the average total travel time
per passenger is minimized if the 35-percentile value is used to determine the scheduled trip time
based on historical data". So, using a trip time value, ensuring that 35% of the bus trips arrive
on time. This low value prevents most buses to depart earlier than the scheduled departure
time, thus saving many passengers much waiting time. To ensure a punctual departure for the
following trip in the opposite direction, layover time should be increased by the same amount
of time, with which the trip time is decreased (Van Oort [2011]). While the trick of preventing
buses to operate ahead of schedule can also be achieved by vehicle holding, vehicle holding can
lead to other implications (see following).
Schedule- or headway-based holding
Probably the most commonly used method is the schedule- or headway-based holding of buses.
The strategy of schedule based holding is characterized by embedding slack time in the schedule
and holding buses at each control point for a period of time before their scheduled departure.
A bus is generally held longer if it is ahead of schedule and shorter if it is behind schedule so
that the travel time variability of the trip to this check stop can be neutralized.
For headway based holding usually threshold rules are introduced in which a bus is held at a
stop if its preceding headway falls below a given threshold (Turnquist and Bowman [1980], Fu
and Yang [2002], Cats [2014]). In a ﬁeld experiment, Cats [2014] enabled bus drivers to drive
in a regular mode by installing a device in the bus. This device communicates to the bus driver
real-time data from the bus ahead and behind. Through this device, the bus driver knows if he
is too close to the bus in front or to the bus behind and can thereupon adjust the speed or hold
at a bus stop. By implementing this indicator the CoV of the headways decreased by 11-26%
(peak and oﬀpeak periods) and bus bunching was reduced by 13-24%. Xuan et al. [2011]
show that dynamic holding strategies based on headways alone cannot help buses adhere to a
schedule. He proposes "a family of dynamic holding strategies that use bus arrival deviations
from a virtual schedule at the control points" (Xuan et al. [2011], p.1831). It requires real-time
information from the current bus and its leading and bus and applies a dynamic holding time
depending on the distance to the leading bus. His research shows that with this approach, both
maintaining schedule adherence and adhering headway regularity without too much slack is
enabled. In fact, the method was found "to require about 40% less slack than the conventional
24
==PAGE:36==
schedule-based method" (Xuan et al. [2011], p.1831).
Schedule Optimization
Recently operations research has been developing algorithms to optimize bus schedules.
A
algorithm by Chen [2014] ﬁnds the optimal number of bus runs in each time period and the ideal
departure time at the origin stop in order to minimize the total waiting time of passengers.The
number of passengers and the arrival pattern is assumed to be known and the developed dynamic
programming algorithm solves the bus scheduling problem.
2.5.3
Improvements on a strategical level
Incentive scheme
Cats [2014] suggests implementing a regularity indicator into the incentive scheme. Today most
contracts already have a bonus or penalty included, if the delivered level of service is not as
agreed upon in the contract. Punctuality (see OTP in chapter 2.1) is an important part of the
level of service. Jansson and Pyddoke [2010] and Cats [2014] however argue that the incentives
related to punctuality are unpredictable and insigniﬁcant, moreover, it also does not reﬂect the
passenger perception of service reliability. Therefore Cats [2014] suggest a regularity indicator
in order to improve service regularity.
Service network design
Line length and line coordination: One way to improve the reliability is to optimize the
line length and line coordination (Lee [2013]). By making routes long one can oﬀer many direct
connections, thereby saving transfers. However, the variability of trip or travel time usually
increases with the length of a line, resulting in worse schedule adherence and additional waiting
time for passengers. Line coordination is applied if (parts of) multiple lines are running in
parallel on the same route. The idea behind is that passengers on that route will have a more
frequent transport service and more direct connections. For example, if two lines on a route
are operated, the headway for passengers can be reduced by 50% if the headway is the same
for both lines and if they are timed correctly (meaning line A arrives in the halfway of the
headway of line B). Van Oort [2011] shows that this is a proper instrument of service reliability
improvement. According to an international survey by Van Oort [2011], the method of line
25
==PAGE:37==
coordination is applied by 71% of the operators.
Bus priority measures: Another way to improve the reliability is to prioritize buses in
the transport system, meaning assigning exclusive bus lanes, stop design and traﬃc signal pri-
orities (Egeter [1995], Van Nes and Bovy [2000], Van Nes [2002]). B.J.Waterson et al. [2011]
takes this concept one step further in his research and examines the behavioral responses of
travellers following the implementation of "strong bus priority measures", where the road ca-
pacity is completely given to buses. The ﬁndings are "ﬁrstly that the eﬀect of implementing
such strong bus priority measures is as dependent on the characteristics of the local travelers
as on the scheme itself and secondly that implementing too strong a scheme may not beneﬁt
public transport overall"(B.J.Waterson et al. [2011], p.642). Nevertheless, research has shown
the positive eﬀects on schedule adherence if buses get exclusive lanes and have stop design and
traﬃc signal priorities (Egeter [1995], Van Nes [2002]).
26
==PAGE:38==
3
Methodology
After presenting the literature in the previous chapter, the methodology used for the data
process, from collecting to plotting, as well as, the used KPIs will be explained.
3.1
Data
The data for this research was provided by MOVIA within the IPTOP project together with
the DTU.
The data was collected by the Voice Data Radio (VDR) equipment in the buses. The VDR
system was rolled out to the bus operations in Greater Copenhagen in MOVIA’s area at the
end of 2006. The system combines traditional verbal radio communication with collection and
communication of real-time data on traﬃc and operation. The buses are equipped with a GPS
receiver, a Tetra radio (Terrestrial Trunked Radio), a small bus PC and a Vehicle Integration
Unit (VIU), which controls communication to and from the bus.
Via the Tetra radio, the
system communicates with the central servers that call directly to the relevant garages and
sends data reports to the Automatic Vehicle Location (AVL) system, which keeps track of the
buses positions. The AVL system communicates with the traﬃc management system and keeps
track of all real-time, forecast and schedule data, such as the traﬃc system processes (Nielsen
[2007]).
The real-time data that was collected by the VDR and sent to the AVL from September 2014
until December 2014 is used for this paper.
3.1.1
Automatic Vehicle Location (AVL) data
The original data sets and information that were provided are shown in ﬁgure 6.
In the JourneyHistory ﬁle, each journey of a bus and all observed and planned arrival and
departure dates and times at the stops, as well as the trip and vehicle number, are recorded. It
is by far the biggest ﬁle. In the Journey Create ﬁle the corresponding info regarding bus line,
journey pattern, timetable, trip direction is given. In the TimedStopList ﬁle, the stop sequence,
the indication of a holding stop (FLAG) and timed pattern are given. In the Vehicles ﬁle, the

Issues with data:
As with almost every data set, also these sets have some implications. The bus operators are
obliged by MOVIA to collect data and the bus driver has a few tasks when logging on to the
system. It is important to log on with the correct line number and trip direction and to keep
the system running for the whole trip. Data, however, shows that at the end of a trip many
values are missing, which can be caused by an early log out in order to be ready for the next
trip. Furthermore, there are a few timestamp errors, where the timestamp at a stop shows an
earlier departure than the arrival timestamp or a later stop shows an earlier timestamp than
one of the previous stops. In the section 3.1.3 Data manipulation, the process of data handling
is explained and in the next section 3.1.2, the subset selection is described.
3.1.2
Data selection
The complete data set contains more than 55 million observations (JourneyHistory) for a total
of 223 bus lines in the period of September, 1st until November 30 in the year of 2014. For
the analysis in this paper several lines have been analyzed but due to the extent of information
and analysis only two bus lines are outlined in this paper. For all other lines a likewise analysis
was or can be carried out as well.
The two selected bus lines are the 150S and the 173E
(today 15E). Bus lines with an "S" attached to the line number, are quick and direct buses with
few stops en route that connect the suburbs with the city. The 150S from Kokkedal St. to
Nørreport St. connects Lyngby, Hørsholm and Kokkedal to Copenhagen, Nørreport St. (the
green circles in ﬁgure 7 indicate the stops). The "E-buses" are express bus lines running mostly
during rush hour. They run from suburbs into the city including major workplace areas or
major transportation hubs. The 173E bus connects Hørsholm (Søhuset) with the city center of
Copenhagen (Nørreport St.). Parts of the routes of these two bus lines overlap. They therefore
partially stop at the same stations, indicated by circles having both colors, green and black in
ﬁgure 7.
The reason for choosing these bus lines is the frequent use of them (150S is amongst top 10 bus
lines by passenger count in 2018 (MOVIA [2019])), the comparability regarding parts of their
29
==PAGE:41==
Figure 7: Map of bus stop of bus line 150S and 173E
route, the diﬀerence in delay and the mix of roads and areas (from suburbs to city using local
roads, highways and main streets and junctions within the city) which is a good representation
of conditions the bus network faces in whole Greater Zealand.

3.1.3
Data manipulation
Several operations have been performed in order to maximize insights from the given data.
Mainly joining, data cleaning and calculations have been performed and thereafter analyzed.
Alteryx is the software that is used to perform these steps. Alteryx is a leader in data science
and self-service analytics with a platform that can prepare, blend, enrich, and analyze data
(Alteryx [2019]).
Joining of data
The Journey History ﬁle with more than 55 million observations was joined with the Journey-
Create ﬁle in order to ﬁlter on selected bus lines which was needed for later calculations. After
joining the two ﬁles and ﬁltering on the two selected bus lines, a total of 613.748 observations
was left. Furthermore, the ﬁle got joined with the TimedStops and StopLocations in order to be
able to show the correct sequence of stops the bus was approaching and to calculate the distance
and speed. Only in the last join, 16 bus stop numbers were not contained in the StopLocations.
These bus stops are points called "VIA" and are control points but not actual stop points and
can be neglected (67.378 observations). The ﬁle had a total of 546,370 observations and after
the cleaning steps in the following section a total of 539,362 observations was left and is used
for the analysis.
31
==PAGE:43==
Data cleaning
Data cleaning consists mainly of the imputation and deleting of data.
The deleting of observations was based on the validity of the timestamps, an error in the AVL
or a false log in of the bus driver onto the system. If there are only one or two valid timestamps,
a false log in was assumed , in which the bus driver accidentally logged onto the wrong journey
and realized it after the ﬁrst or second bus stop.
For the imputation of data there are multiple ways, such as ignoring (deleting) it, using the
most frequent or constant values, taking the mean or median or k nearest neighbours (Herlau
et al. [2018]). There is however not a lot of literature on imputating missing date values (most
literature is for factor or numeric values) and therefore the two following approaches are used
to impute the missing data. First the average dwell time of each bus stop number (each bus
stop has two stop numbers, one for each rip direction) was calculated. If the observation had a
invalid Observed Arrival Date and a valid Observed Departure Date, the average dwell time of
that stop was deducted from the Observed Departure Date. If the observation contained NULL
values in both ﬁelds (Observed Arrival/Departure Date) the mean between the two nearest
neighbours, meaning the observations of the previous and following stop of that journey are
used to impute the missing date value. In total 178,406 observed arrival and departure date
(including timestamp) values in 105,095 observations were imputed. For an overview see table
2 in the following page.
Moreover the ﬁle had 153,374 observations with at least one missing value. This represents 28%
of the observations of the used data set. Through imputation 69% (105,095) of the observations
with missing values could be imputed. Of the remaining 48,279 observations, 17,214 observa-
tions in 990 journeys are cancelled journeys/trips (no valid timestamp at all). The remaining
31,065 observations (less than 6% of the complete data set) are randomly distributed across
the data set and are not imputed and not used for the analysis.
It was striking that most missing values were at the end of the route. The following ﬁgure 9
shows the sequence of stops, from ﬁrst until last stop and the number of NULL values in the
observed departure date (y-axis).
Almost 32% of all missing data is due to the last stop (Kokkedal St., Norreport St. or Naerum

Calculations:
To enrich the quality of the data set several steps have been taken in order to be able to
calculate the relevant KPIs.
• Calculating dwell time at stops
• Calculating delay (deviation between planned and observed departure / arrival) and OTP
• Deﬁning peak
• Calculating headway (observed and scheduled)
• Deﬁning bus bunching (for stops and bus lines)
• Calculating observed and scheduled run times
• Calculating the layover time
• Calculating average passenger wait times
• Calculating average passenger travel times
A detailed list of the manipulations steps and calculations is provided in the appendix D.
Final data set and model:
The ﬁles Vehicles and Stops contained 559 respectively 198 duplicates, which got removed. The
ﬁle StopLocations did not contain any errors in the data. The ﬁle TripTimeAnalysis and Lay-
overAnalysis were created based on the JourneyHistory_TimedStopList_JourneyCreate_StopList.
The observed and planned run time are contained in the TripTimeAnalysis ﬁle and the lay-
over time in minutes and in percent of the run time are contained in the LayoverAnalysis ﬁle.
This information is saved in separate ﬁles because the run and layover time is only useful per
JourneyID and not per stop or recordID.

3.2
Measures and key performance indicators of reliability used in
this study
In the following sections one or multiple of the presented equations from chapter 2, literature
review, are chosen to calculate the relevant KPIs and adapted to the analysis to the MOVIA
data.
3.2.1
On-Time Performance: Schedule adherence
Schedule adherence will be measured in two ways. One measure describes the schedule adher-
ence as a percentage of journeys that are within in the deﬁned "on-time" bandwidth by MOVIA,
which is a departure of one minute early to ﬁve minutes late (at timing point places), using
equation 1. This shall give a good indication of the OTP of the bus line.
To calculate the average delay of a bus line at a stop, a slight adaption of the equation of
2 is applied. While Hansen [1999] neglects the fact of early and late departures by taking
the absolute values of the deviation, this research also looks into the negative delays, so early
departures. This is speciﬁcally interesting when looking into delay per stop (see chapter 4.3).

MOVIA deﬁnes good regularity as the distance between two buses at a given place that does
not exceed the planned interval plus ﬁve minutes.

The dwell time is simply calculated as the time diﬀerence from arriving to departing from a

3.2.4
Run time variability
The run time variability shows the diﬀerent values it takes a bus to complete a route. To derive
the run time, the total trip time from departure at the ﬁrst stop to the arrival at the last stop
is taken. This is done for observed and planned trip times. A box plot will be used to show
the variability in run times.
38
==PAGE:50==
3.2.5
Bus bunching
While there is no unique way in the literature about the identiﬁcation of bus bunching most
literature only considers bus bunching along bus lines. In this paper, this perspective shall
be extended to a bus stop perspective. From a bus stop perspective bus bunching in its most
extreme form is analyzed, meaning that if buses arrive directly after each other at the bus stop
or if they are queuing to approach the bus stop. The approach by Feng and Figliozzi [2011] (see
section 3.2.5) is used for this measure and the time limit is set to 10 seconds (see equation 18).
Therefore, if a bus arrives 10 seconds after a previous bus (from any other line) has departed
it is assumed that the bus had to wait to approach the bus stop or terminal and bus bunching
occurs. It should, however, be noted that at some bus stops more than one bus can stop at a
time, therefore this analysis might not be suitable for all bus stops. In this research, the bus
stop size was taken into account as far as it was known.

3.2.6
Average passenger wait time
Bus line 173E runs mostly on a high-frequency basis and therefore the average expected passen-
ger waiting time will be calculated according to equation 9 only using data collected during the
hours of the day where it operates on a high-frequency basis (07:00, 15:00 and 16:00) between
Nærum St. and Nørreport St..
39
==PAGE:51==
Bus line 150S is operated depending on the time of the day as headway or schedule based. In
order to compare the two bus lines the passenger wait time will also be calculated on the basis
of equation 9 for the low-frequency hours and route (between Nærum St. and Nørreport St.).
For bus line 150S the headway is ﬁve minutes, while bus line 173E operates with a ten-minute
headway.
For the same time periods, the additional waiting time per passenger will be calculated using
equation 10.
3.2.7
Average passenger travel time
The passenger travel time will be calculated using the expected passenger waiting time and the
drive time in the bus (run time) until alighting at the ﬁnal stop. The equations for average
expected passenger travel time (equation 11) and average additional passenger travel time
(equation 14) will be used.
40
==PAGE:52==
4
Results and benchmarking of Metro Copenhagen Bus
Transport Lines
In this chapter, the results of the analysis of the various KPIs are presented.
The analysis is done for two selected bus lines. The criteria for the selection is already explained
in section 3.1.2 and is only brieﬂy repeated:
• Bus line 150S (Kokkedal St. - Nørreport St.) is among the bus lines with the highest
average delay (see ﬁgure 8) and one of the top 10 bus lines in terms of number of passengers
• Bus line 173E (Søhuset, Forskerparken - Nørreport St.) is running partially on the same
route with a lower delay and is therefore good for benchmarking (black stops)
The bus line 173E was renamed to line 15E after a change in 2016. It still serves the same route
and only the name was changed. In August 2014 the bridges for Øverødvej and Gøngehusvej
were demolished. This meant that the short trips for bus lines 150S and 173E to Gammel
Holte, Øverødvej temporarily changed until January 3, 2016 in readjusting the route from
Nærum st. along Rundforbivej - Egebækvej - Malmbergsvej with the end station at Gammel
Holte, Mariehøjvej. In this research, the short trip will be considered to end at Nærum St.
since the detour was only temporarily.
Figure 12: Map of selected bus lines in Greater Copenhagen
41
==PAGE:53==
Both bus lines operate a long and a short route. For bus line 150S the long route is from
Kokkedal St. to Nørreport St. and the short route is from Nærum St. to Nørreport St. and
vice versa (in this paper from Nærum St. due to the re-routing, otherwise it would be from Gl.
Holte, Øverødvej). For bus line 173E the same concept applies and the long route is run from
Søhuset, Forskerparken to Nørreport St. and the short route from Nærum St. to Nørreport St.
and vice versa.

==PAGE:54==
4.1
On-Time Performance: Schedule adherence
As already described in section 2.3 the OTP measure for low-frequency bus services is described
as schedule adherence and is measured by the punctuality of arrivals and/or departures at stops.
MOVIA deﬁnes a service that is within the range of one minute early to ﬁve minutes late (at
timing point places) as punctual. Furthermore, MOVIA operates some bus lines as a high-
frequency bus line during the day or rush hours and low-frequency in the rest of the day (see
ﬁgure 13 and 14).
Similar to other research, the analysis here focuses primarily on departure times/delays.
Figure 13: Schedule for bus line 150S (high-frequency service marked by box)
Figure 14: Schedule for bus line 173E (high-frequency service marked by box)
43
==PAGE:55==
4.1.1
On-Time Performance: Schedule adherence classic method
Figure 15 shows the average departure delay and the OTP for the selected bus lines during
their low frequency phases in a day.
Figure 15: Average delay and OTP of selected bus lines (at low-frequency service hours)
During the low frequency-hours of the day, the bus line 150S has an average delay of 2 minutes
and 57 seconds (2.95 minutes) at its holding stops and an OTP of 73%. Bus line 173E has an
average delay of 1 minute and 58 seconds (1.97 minutes) at its holding stops and an OTP of
68%. To benchmark these values to other international cities, the OTP target for Washington
(WMATA) is above 79% and for Boston (MBTA) it is 80% for "Key Bus(es)" and 75% for non
"Key Bus(es)". The actual observed OTP in 2016 was 74% in Washington and 65% in Boston
(wmata [2017], MBTA [2017b]). Therefore the values in Copenhagen are below the target but
in between the performance of other cities. It should be noted that the thresholds for these
cities are diﬀerent (see chapter 2).
A more in-depth analysis of bus line 150S shows the delay and OTP per holding stop for each
trip direction (td) in the following section 4.1.2.

4.1.3
On-Time Performance: Delays above ﬁve minutes
Looking at the delays above ﬁve minutes, it shall be examined how late a bus is if it is considered
late. Passengers usually remember the time the bus got canceled or had a huge delay and
perceive it as regular events. This analysis shall show if these long delays are rare occasions
or if they are regular events. With a boxplot, this shall be visualized. If the delays are close
to the ﬁve-minute threshold, then the assumption of regular long delays can be neglected. But
if the box is considerably higher than the ﬁve-minute threshold the passengers’ perceptions is
proven right.
The following boxplot (ﬁgure 20) shows all delays above 5 min.
At almost every stop a few observations with delays of more than one hour can be seen but for
all stops (except for Frederiksborgvej more than 75% of delays are (far) below the 20 minutes.
In the following ﬁgures, the delays per bus lines and td shall be analyzed. The upper limit of
y-axis in the plot is set to 40 minutes for visual reasons.

4.2
On-Time Performance: Headway irregularity
4.2.1
On-Time Performance: Headway regularity using a bandwidth
MOVIA deﬁnes good headway regularity as the "distance between two buses at a given place
that does not exceed the planned interval plus ﬁve minutes" (MOVIA [2015], p.2).
For this analysis only weekdays and the hours of the day where the bus lines are operated
in a high-frequency service are considered. For both bus lines the route from Nørreport St.
to Nærum St. and back, which is operated with a 5 minute (150S), respectively ten-minute
headway (173E) is observed. Looking at this subset the OTP is 87,3% for bus line 150S and
82,9% for bus line 173E.
This KPI measures the regularity and not the punctuality, which shall be shown in the following
example.
Figure 25: Departure times of bus line 150S at bus stop Ryparken St.
In ﬁgure 25, only one bus leaves on time (OTP_Departure = True) but all of them run regular
(Headway_Regularity = True).
The ﬁrst bus runs 6 minutes late, but in a regular mode
according to MOVIA (within the planned headway plus ﬁve minutes). This example shall show
and emphasize the importance of regularity and not punctuality for high-frequency buses.
4.2.2
On-Time Performance: Headway irregularity classic methods
Next to the OTP measure used by MOVIA, the CoV (see equation 3) and the PRDM (see
equation 4) are measures that are used in theory and practice.
The CoV is the standard
deviation of the headways compared to the mean of headways. The indicator PRDM expresses
the average deviation of actual headways compared to the scheduled ones.
51
==PAGE:63==
Before explaining the results in ﬁgure 26 to 29 a small example is provided to illustrate the
meaning of PRDM and CoV. If the PRDM is 0% it means that the bus(es) depart completely
regular. If the bus has a headway of 10 minutes and leaves 1 minute early or late the PRDM is
10%. For the CoV, a similar theory applies but is only applicable for a multitude of observations
where it compares the standard deviation of observed headways to the average planned headway.
For e.g. 3 buses depart from Nærum St. with a planned headway of 10 minutes. The observed
headways of departures are 7, 10 and 12 minutes, hence the average (avg.) of the observed
headway is 9.6 minutes, the standard deviation (std.) is 2.1 and the CoV is 0.21.
All these values are displayed in the ﬁgures 26-29 and analyzed thereafter.
Bus line 150S
Figure 26: Avg., Std., CoV, PRDM and OTP of Headway - Bus line 150S td 1
In ﬁgure 26 the CoV is 0.72, the PRDM 50.5% and the OTP measure according to MOVIA
(OTP_HF) is 94.2%. The performance measure used by MOVIA is misleading since a journey
with a planned headway of 5 minutes is allowed to arrive after 10 minutes (twice the headway)
and is still considered as "on time". Therefore the PRDM and the CoV are more representative
of the regularity of the service. According to the PRDM the actual headways are on average
2.5 minutes oﬀ(either lower or higher than) the planned headway. Also, a CoV of 0.72 is not
a good result for regularity. Looking at the example above (ﬁgure 25) this is seen as well. The
headway ranges between 1 and 10 minutes but never takes the value of the planned headway,
hence a big CoV. The irregularity increases with the trip distance, which could be due to the
trip distance and/or due to the fact that the bus is entering the city and is more exposed to
external factors such as congestion and traﬃc lights.
52
==PAGE:64==
Figure 27: Avg., Std., CoV, PRDM and OTP of Headway - Bus line 150S td 2
Looking at td 2 a similar picture is seen. According to MOVIA’s OTP measure buses perform
well at 92.6%, but the CoV of 0.79 and PRDM of 57.6% are clearly indicating an irregular
service. Rigshospitalet Syd has very few observations, and apart from this stop a decrease in
regularity with an increase trip distance is seen.
Bus line 173E
Figure 28: Avg., Std., CoV, PRDM and OTP of Headway - Bus line 173E td 1
Bus line 173E performs according to the MOVIA standard (OTP of 83.4%) worse than the bus
line 150S, but looking at the CoV (0.54) and PRDM (37.5%) a better regularity is seen. This is
because the average headway is higher than for bus lines 150S, the planned headway is 10 and
not 5 minutes. Furthermore, the service seems to be most regular in the middle of the route
(CoV: 0.47) while in the beginning (CoV: 0.56) and in the end (CoV: 0.61) the service is less
regular.
53
==PAGE:65==
Figure 29: Avg., Std., CoV, PRDM and OTP of Headway - Bus line 173E td 2
For td 2 the CoV is 0.58, PRDM 49.7% and OTP 77.4%. The PRDM is for all stops approxi-
mately the same until the last stop where it jumps to 56.4%.
54
==PAGE:66==
4.3
Trip analysis along the route
4.3.1
Trip analysis along the route
In order to get more insights about the delays and to analyze if certain stops or parts of the
route contribute more to the delay than others, a detailed route analysis along the stops is
carried out. A delay at a single bus stop is the diﬀerence between the scheduled departure
time and the actual departure time. A negative delay indicates that the bus is ahead of the
schedule. This is done for every stop along a route for the given bus lines. Patterns respectively
stops or segments can be detected, that are contributing or recovering delays. The trips during
low-frequency hours for both, the long and short route are examined in this analysis.
In the following graphs, the delay is shown in minutes and the color code is to diﬀerentiate
between holding stop points (black) and regular stop points (green).
Busline 150S - Direction 1: Kokkedal St. to Nørreport St.
Figure 30: Bus departure delays along a route (Bus line 150S - td 1)
In ﬁgure 30 several delay jumps can be detected. A jump is deﬁned as a delay of more than
+/- one minute to the previous stop. Jumps in delay can be seen at the stops Kokkedal St.,
Dr. Neergaardsvej, Nærum St. and Haraldsgade marked by a box around the delay.
As already mentioned in the previous section 4.1.2, it is striking that delays are evident from
the ﬁrst stop onward. To ﬁnd an explanation of these jumps, an analysis of the dwell time
(ﬁgure 31), the arrival time (ﬁgure 32), and over time (per Peak (ﬁgure 33)) is done.
55
==PAGE:67==
Figure 31: Dwell time (in seconds) at stops along a route (Bus line 150S - td 1)
Figure 32: Bus arrival delays along a route (Bus line 150S - td 1)
Figure 31 and 32 show that buses arrive early at their ﬁrst stop but due to their long dwell
time of more than 3 minutes (195 seconds) buses depart with a delay.
Looking at the stops Dr. Neergaardsvej and Haraldsgade the dwell time is low and the arrival
at these stops is already delayed. Therefore, it is assumed, that the delay is caused by the
driving time. The stop Haraldsgade is right after an intersection and unfortunate traﬃc signal
coordination could be an explanation for the increase in delay. The negative jump in delay at
Nærum St. could be explained by the fact it being a holding stop, hence a bigger buﬀer time
is included at this stop which helps to recover some of the delay. However, the holding time
is not represented in the schedule where the arrival and departure times are the same. It is,
however, assumed that the buﬀer time is incorporated in the departure time. The dwell time
56
==PAGE:68==
of 60 seconds is plausible, since this stop connects to the train and multiple other bus lines and
therefore it is assumed that many people are boarding and alighting. Towards the end of the
route some of the delay is recovered and from ﬁgure 32 it is evident that it is recovered in the
driving time (delay in arrival time decreases).
Looking into delays over time, the morning peak is the biggest inﬂuence on the overall delay,
especially for the part of the route when the bus enters the city at the end of the route. The
delay is slowly and progressively building up (see ﬁgure 33).

4.3.2
Layover time
After discovering that buses of both lines in both directions on average depart late it was
assumed that buses had too little layover time. Layover time is the time a bus has between
ﬁnishing his run and starting the return run. An analysis of the layover time in percent of the
run time has been done (see ﬁgure 50).
More than 70% of all runs have a layover time of less than 50% of the run time. It is clear that
the less layover time there is, the higher the average delay at the departure from the origin
stop. If the minimum layover time would be increased to 10% (of the planned run time) the
64
==PAGE:76==
Figure 50: Layover time in percent of planned run time
average delay at the origin stop would decrease by 13%, from 2.45 minutes to 2.13.
65
==PAGE:77==
4.4
Dwell time
The dwell time is the time that is used for boarding and alighting of passengers at a stop.
Figure 51 shows the dwell time at stops for the bus lines 150S and 173E where the bus actually
stopped, so a dwell time minimum of one second (and an upper limit on the y-axis is set to 100
seconds for visual reasons). It is diﬀerentiated between holding stops (x-axis label: True) and
regular stops (x-axis label: False) and between diﬀerent peaks. The morning peak is deﬁned
from 7:00 until 9:00 and the afternoon peak from 15:00 until 17:00. All other times of the day
are grouped in No Peak. A holding stop implies stopping a vehicle if it runs before the schedule
at the stop point.
Figure 51: Dwell time of buses at timed holding points (true/false) and peak
The median and variance of dwell time of the holding stops is greater than of the regular stops.
However, the peak seems to not aﬀect the variance in dwell time too much. In the following, the
holding stop points per bus line will be analyzed in greater detail. Furthermore, in the following
ﬁgures (52 and 53) the upper limit of dwell time is set to 100 seconds for visual reasons. The
observations above 100 seconds will be analyzed later in this section.
Figure 52 shows the greatest variance in dwell time for the stations Hans Knudsen Plads,
Kokkedal St. and Nørreport St.. Kokkedal St. and Nørreport St. are the origin (and ﬁnal)
stops of bus line 150S which explains the high variance, since buses usually dwell long at the
start of their trip and only little at the end of their trip (usually the driver checks out as soon
as he arrives, therefore a dwell time of 0 or just a few seconds is seen at the ﬁnal stop). The
66
==PAGE:78==
Figure 52: Dwell time of bus line 150S at its holding points (Upper dwell time limit 100 seconds)
on average long and high variance in dwell time for the stop Hans Knudsen Plads is mainly
inﬂuenced by td 2 (towards Kokkedal St.) where the bus stop is in front of a big intersection
with traﬃc lights, which causes the bus to stop longer than needed and the high variance.
Figure 53 shows the greatest median and variance for Nørreport St. and Søhuset, Forskerparken,
which are the origin (and ﬁnal) stops for bus line 173E and as explained above usually have a
dwell long at the start of their trip and only little at the end of their trip.
Figure 53: Dwell time of bus line 173E at its holding points (Upper dwell time limit 100 seconds)
Investigating the dwell times above 100 seconds on a single journey basis it appears that in most
cases, long dwell times are caused by an early arrival and the holding strategy which permits
the buses to leave early. The Deviation Arrival (in ﬁgure 54) describes the time diﬀerence of
the observed and the planned bus arrival at a stop (in minutes) and it shows that buses are
arriving a long time before their scheduled arrival time. The Planned and Observed Arrival
Date as well as the ObservedDwellTime(in seconds) is displayed for a better understanding.
67
==PAGE:79==
Kokkedal St. in td 1, the origin stop has the most and longest dwell times. This means that

and long dwell times (ﬁgure 55). Observation eight at Lundofteparken is the only exception
where the bus arrives late by more than half an hour and dwells long. Looking at this trip,
the bus registers its ﬁrst stop at Lundofteparken. Furthermore also the previous and following

4.5
Run time variability
Run time variability describes the variance it takes a bus to complete one trip. Diﬀerences in
passenger loads and season have eﬀects on the average bus run times over the year by month,
week and day.
The long trip (150S: Kokkedal St.
to Nørreport St.
and 173E: Søhuset, Forskerparken to
Nørreport St.) in both directions for each bus line will be analyzed. Looking at the bus lines
over time on a monthly basis the mean run time of bus line 150S is decreasing with every month
by one minutes from 54 minutes in September to 52 minutes in November. For bus line 173E
exactly the same pattern is seen, where the mean run time is 41 minutes in September and 39
minutes in November.
Figure 58: Box plot of Run time for the month of September to November for selected bus lines
After looking at it on a monthly basis a more granular analysis is done depending on the day in
the week. For bus line 150S the weekend shows diﬀerent mean and median run times of 51 and
50 minutes compared to 53 minutes during the week. For bus line 173E the mean and median
run time vary between 39 and 40 minutes during the week.
To analyze in even greater detail, run times were analyzed depending on peaks. Peaks were
deﬁned as a morning peak (from 7:00 to 9:00) and an afternoon peak (from 15:00 to 17:00) and
all other times in a day are deﬁned as no peak.
Already visually a signiﬁcant diﬀerence in run time depending on the peak is seen. To back
this up, the non-parametric Kruskal Wallis test was used for testing the null hypothesis of
70
==PAGE:82==
Figure 59: Box plot of Run time for the days in a week for selected bus lines
Figure 60: Observed Run time distribution in peaks for given bus lines
equal means for the observed run times across the diﬀerent peaks. This test rejects the null
hypothesis and thus there exist diﬀerent means depending on the time of the day. Before, the
Anderson-Darling normality test has shown a non-normal distribution of the data. This test
was chosen since the data set contains more observations than the shapiro test in R can handle.
Both peaks seem to heavily inﬂuence the run times of bus line 150S. The mean is 55, respectively
56 minutes, compared to 52 minutes in no peak. For bus line 173E the median is approximately
the same for all peaks, but looking at the mean value the morning rush hour seems to be the
main inﬂuence. In the morning the mean is 42 minutes while in the no and afternoon peak the
mean run time is 39 minutes. The exact run time values (in minutes) for the bus lines and the
corresponding peaks are provided in table 4. PM is the abbreviation for afternoon and AM the
abbreviation for morning.

St. all the way to Søhuset, Forskerparken in the morning and in the afternoon. The observed
run time, however, is changing and higher run times are seen in the peaks of the day while
lower run times are seen in the rest of the day. In general, the average planned run time is 35
minutes and the average observed run time is 37.2 minutes.

4.6
Bus bunching
As described in section 3.2.5, bus bunching is analyzed from two perspectives, the bus stop and
the bus line perspective.
First, the bus bunching at stops is analyzed in order to detect stops were buses had to wait
before approaching the bus stop. Bus bunching at stops is detected if a bus is arriving at a
stop within a time interval of less than 10 seconds after a previous bus (from any other line)
has departed.
In the following, the bus stops are ranked by the absolute frequency (total number) of bunched
buses (ﬁgure 65). Bus bunching (in %) is the relative measure of the number of bunched buses
versus the number of all buses that stopped at the given stop.
Figure 65: Bus bunching at stops (ordered by the total number of bunched buses)
Most bus bunching happens at the stops within the city where the stops are shared by both
bus lines, which increases the chance of bus bunching.
Secondly, bus bunching from the bus line perspective is analyzed.
The value of bus bunching is given in percent of the total observations of the bus line, meaning
that at 6.63% of all observations of bus line 150S bus bunching occurs. For bus line 173E bus
bunching occurs 2.11% of the time.
75
==PAGE:87==
4.7
Average passenger wait time
The reliability measures that have been discussed so far in sections 4.1 to 4.6 in this paper are
focused on KPIs from a public transport perspective. The results of the KPIs in the above
sections measure the performance of the buses and misses to capture the eﬀects of irregular
service on passengers. However, this section and section 4.8 look at the eﬀects of irregular
service on passengers, namely passenger waiting time and passenger travel time.
As discussed in section 2.3, the average additional waiting time is the indicator used to measure
wait time reliability. The wait time variability is calculated for high-frequency hours of the day
for both bus lines. Therefore, the assumption of random passenger arrival pattern at original
stops was considered.
Figures 66 to 69 show the average waiting times for each bus line and the holding stops in its
corresponding td.
Bus line 150S
Figure 66: Average ideal, actual and additional waiting time - Bus line 150S td 1
Figure 66 shows the ideal, actual and additional waiting time per stop for trips during high-
frequency service hours between Nærum St. and Nørreport St.. The headway for this route is 5
minutes, therefore the ideal wait time is 2.5 minutes. The average additional wait time is 1.06
minutes resulting in an average actual wait time of 3.56 minutes.
The same route and hours of the day are chosen for td 2. Therefore the headway in ﬁgure 67
is also 5 minutes and the ideal wait time is 2.5 minutes. The average actual wait time is 3.83
minutes.
76
==PAGE:88==
Figure 67: Average ideal, actual and additional waiting time - Bus line 150S td 2
Bus line 173E
Figure 68: Average ideal, actual and additional waiting time - Bus line 173E td 1
For bus line 173E the same trip constraints are chosen, namely trips during high-frequency
service hours between Nærum St. and Nørreport St.. The headway for this route is 10 minutes,
therefore the ideal wait time is 5 minutes. The average additional wait time is 1.56 minutes
resulting in an actual wait time of 6.56 minutes.
Figure 69: Average ideal, actual and additional waiting time - Bus line 173E td 2
For td 2 the average actual wait time is 6.96 minutes and the average additional wait time is
1.96 minutes.

4.8
Average passenger travel time
Passenger travel time is the sum of waiting and run/trip time. As discussed in section 2.3,
travel time represents the observed trip time plus the actual waiting time. Additional travel
time captures the extra time spent compared to the planned waiting and trip time. In this
section, the results of the analysis of passenger travel time is shown.
Bus line 150S
In ﬁgure 70 the planned and actual passenger travel time, as well as the ideal and actual
waiting times, are shown.
The trips are the short trips during the high-frequency service
between Nærum St. and Nørreport St.. The diﬀerences in the planned travel time are due to
the diﬀerent run times depending on the hour of the day (diﬀerent in the morning and in the
afternoon). The ideal waiting time is 2.5 minutes and the average planned travel time is at
around 35 minutes.
Figure 70: Actual and planned wait and travel time - Bus line 150S td 1
Figure 71 shows that on average the additional travel time is 0.45 minutes. Only one observation
with an exceptional high additional travel time of 33 minutes is observed.
Looking at td 2 a higher variance in travel times is seen. The planned ideal waiting time is 2.5
minutes and the planned travel time is at 32 to 33 minutes.
Td 2 shows an additional travel time of 1.85 minutes.
